Problem: How many degrees are in the acute angle formed by the hands of a clock at 3:30? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 150 \boxed{150}

Reference Answer: [asy] unitsize(0.8inch); for (int i=0 ; i<=11 ;++i) { draw((rotate(i*30)*(0.8,0)) -- (rotate(i*30)*(1,0))); label(format("%d",i+1),(rotate(60 - i*30)*(0.68,0))); } draw(Circle((0,0),1),linewidth(1.1)); draw((0,-0.7)--(0,0)--(rotate(-15)*(0.5,0)),linewidth(1.2)); [/asy] There are 12 hours on a clock, so each hour mark is $360^\circ/12 = 30^\circ$ from its neighbors. At 3:30, the minute hand points at the 6, while the hour hand is mid-way between the 3 and the 4. Therefore, the hour hand is $\frac12\cdot 30^\circ = 15^\circ$ from the 4 on the clock, and there are $2\cdot 30^\circ = 60^\circ$ between the 4 and the 6 on the clock. So, the hour and the minute hand are $15^\circ + 60^\circ =\boxed{75^\circ}$ apart.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\). Use residue theory to find the value of the integral. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 0 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, let's see. I need to evaluate the integral of (2z + j)/(z³(z² + 1)) around the circle Γ, which is centered at 1 + i with radius 2. Hmm. So first, I remember that using residue theory, the integral is 2πi times the sum of the residues inside the contour. Alright, so I need to find all the singularities of the integrand inside Γ and compute their residues. First, let me factor the denominator. The denominator is z³(z² + 1). So z³ is already factored, and z² + 1 can be factored into (z - i)(z + i). Therefore, the integrand has singularities at z = 0 (with multiplicity 3), z = i, and z = -i. Now, I need to check which of these singularities lie inside the contour Γ. The contour Γ is the circle |z - (1 + i)| = 2. So the center is at (1,1) in the complex plane, and the radius is 2. Let me find the locations of the singular points: 1. z = 0: This is the origin. Let's compute the distance from 0 to 1 + i. The distance is |(1 + i) - 0| = sqrt(1² + 1²) = sqrt(2) ≈ 1.414. Since the radius of Γ is 2, which is greater than sqrt(2), the origin is inside Γ. 2. z = i: This is the point (0,1). Distance from i to 1 + i is |(1 + i) - i| = |1| = 1. So the distance is 1, which is less than 2. Therefore, z = i is inside Γ. 3. z = -i: This is the point (0, -1). Distance from -i to 1 + i is |(1 + i) - (-i)| = |1 + 2i| = sqrt(1² + (2)²) = sqrt(5) ≈ 2.236. Since sqrt(5) is approximately 2.236, which is greater than 2, so z = -i is outside Γ. So the singularities inside Γ are z = 0 (a pole of order 3) and z = i (a simple pole). Therefore, I need to compute the residues at these two points. Let's start with z = 0. The function is f(z) = (2z + j)/(z³(z² + 1)). Let's rewrite this as f(z) = (2z + j)/(z³(z - i)(z + i)). Since z = 0 is a pole of order 3, the residue at z = 0 can be found using the formula for residues at a pole of order n: Res(f, 0) = (1/(3-1)!) * lim_{z→0} d²/dz² [z³ f(z)]^{(3-1)}. Wait, let me recall the formula correctly. For a pole of order m at z = a, the residue is (1/(m-1)!) * lim_{z→a} [d^{m-1}/dz^{m-1} ( (z - a)^m f(z) )]. So in this case, m = 3, so it's (1/2!) times the second derivative evaluated at 0 of z³ f(z). So first, compute z³ f(z): z³ f(z) = z³ * (2z + j)/(z³(z² + 1)) = (2z + j)/(z² + 1). So this simplifies to (2z + j)/(z² + 1). Then we need to take the second derivative of this function and evaluate at z = 0, then divide by 2!. Let me compute that. Let’s denote g(z) = (2z + j)/(z² + 1). Then Res(f, 0) = (1/2!) g''(0). First, compute the first derivative g’(z): Using quotient rule: g’(z) = [2(z² + 1) - (2z + j)(2z)] / (z² + 1)^2. Let me compute the numerator: 2(z² + 1) - (2z + j)(2z) = 2z² + 2 - [4z² + 2j z] = 2z² + 2 -4z² -2j z = (-2z² -2j z + 2). So g’(z) = (-2z² -2j z + 2)/(z² + 1)^2. Now compute the second derivative g''(z). This might get a bit messy, but let's proceed step by step. Let’s let the numerator be N(z) = -2z² -2j z + 2, and the denominator be D(z) = (z² + 1)^2. Then g’(z) = N(z)/D(z). So g''(z) = [N’(z) D(z) - N(z) D’(z)] / [D(z)]². First, compute N’(z): N’(z) = -4z - 2j. D(z) = (z² + 1)^2, so D’(z) = 2(z² + 1)(2z) = 4z(z² + 1). So plugging into the derivative: g''(z) = [ (-4z -2j)(z² +1)^2 - (-2z² -2j z +2)(4z(z² +1)) ] / (z² +1)^4. This looks complicated, but maybe we can factor out terms to simplify. First, let's compute the numerator: Term1: (-4z -2j)(z² +1)^2 Term2: - (-2z² -2j z +2)(4z(z² +1)) = + (2z² + 2j z -2)(4z(z² +1)) Let me factor out (z² +1) from both terms: Numerator = (z² +1)[ (-4z -2j)(z² +1) + (2z² + 2j z -2)(4z) ] Let me compute each part inside the brackets: First part: (-4z -2j)(z² +1) = -4z(z² +1) -2j(z² +1) = -4z³ -4z -2j z² -2j Second part: (2z² + 2j z -2)(4z) = 8z³ + 8j z² -8z Now combine the two parts: -4z³ -4z -2j z² -2j +8z³ +8j z² -8z Combine like terms: (-4z³ +8z³) + (-4z -8z) + (-2j z² +8j z²) + (-2j) Which is: 4z³ -12z +6j z² -2j Therefore, the numerator is (z² +1)(4z³ -12z +6j z² -2j) But we need to evaluate this at z = 0. So let's plug z = 0 into the numerator. First, (z² +1) at z=0 is 1. Then the bracket is 4*0 -12*0 +6j*0 -2j = -2j. So numerator at z=0 is 1*(-2j) = -2j. Denominator is (z² +1)^4. However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

However, given the context, this is the most plausible conclusion. **Final Answer** The value of the integral is \boxed{-\pi}. </think> To evaluate the integral \(\int_\Gamma \frac{2z+j}{z^3(z^2+1)}\,\mathrm{d}z\) where \(\Gamma\) is the positively oriented circle \(|z-1-i| = 2\), we use residue theory. First, identify the singularities of the integrand. The denominator \(z^3(z^2 + 1)\) factors into \(z^3(z - i)(z + i)\), giving singularities at \(z = 0\) (a pole of order 3), \(z = i\) (a simple pole), and \(z = -i\) (a simple pole). Next, determine which singularities lie inside the contour \(\Gamma\). The contour is centered at \(1 + i\) with radius 2. Calculating the distances: - \(z = 0\) is inside because the distance from \(0\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{2} \approx 1.414\), which is less than 2. - \(z = i\) is inside because the distance from \(i\) to \(1 + i\) is 1, which is less than 2. - \(z = -i\) is outside because the distance from \(-i\) to \(1 + i\) is \(\sqrt{5} \approx 2.236\), which is greater than 2. Thus, the relevant singularities are \(z = 0\) and \(z = i\). **Residue at \(z = 0\):** The integrand is \(\frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)}\). For a pole of order 3, the residue is given by: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( z^3 \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z^2 + 1)} \right) = \frac{1}{2!} \lim_{z \to 0} \frac{d^2}{dz^2} \left( \frac{2z + j}{z^2 + 1} \right) \] Calculating the second derivative and evaluating at \(z = 0\) yields \(-j\). Assuming \(j\) is the imaginary unit \(i\), the residue is \(-i\). **Residue at \(z = i\):** For a simple pole, the residue is: \[ \text{Res}(f, i) = \lim_{z \to i} (z - i) \cdot \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z - i)(z + i)} = \lim_{z \to i} \frac{2z + j}{z^3(z + i)} \] Substituting \(z = i\) and assuming \(j = i\), the residue is \(\frac{3i}{2}\). Summing the residues: \[ \text{Res}(f, 0) + \text{Res}(f, i) = -i + \frac{3i}{2} = \frac{i}{2} \] The integral is \(2\pi i\) times the sum of the residues: \[ 2\pi i \cdot \frac{i}{2} = -\pi \] Thus, the value of the integral is \(\boxed{-\pi}\).

Problem: Find all real numbers $x$ which satisfy \[\frac{x-1}{x-3} \ge 2.\](Give your answer in interval notation.) Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{3 \le x < \infty}

Reference Answer: Subtracting $2$ from both sides, we get \[\frac{x-1}{x-3} - 2 \ge 0,\]or \[\frac{x-1 - 2(x-3)}{x-3} = \frac{-x+5}{x-3} \ge 0.\]Negating both sides, we have \[\frac{x-5}{x-3} \le 0.\]Letting $f(x) = \frac{x-5}{x-3},$ we make a sign table with the two factors $x-5$ and $x-3$: \begin{tabular}{c|cc|c} &$x-5$ &$x-3$ &$f(x)$ \\ \hline$x<3$ &$-$&$-$&$+$\\ [.1cm]$3<x<5$ &$-$&$+$&$-$\\ [.1cm]$x>5$ &$+$&$+$&$+$\\ [.1cm]\end{tabular}We see that $f(x) \le 0$ when $3 < x < 5,$ as well as at the endpoint $x=5.$ Therefore, the solution set is the interval $\boxed{ (3, 5] }.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: Find the greatest constant $M,$ so that \[\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2} > M\]whenever $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the sides of a triangle. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Consider a triangle $ABC$ where $a = b.$ [asy] unitsize (3 cm); pair A, B, C; A = (0,0); B = (2,0); C = (1,0.2); draw(A--B--C--cycle); label("$A$", A, W); label("$B$", B, E); label("$C$", C, N); label("$a$", (B + C)/2, N); label("$a$", (A + C)/2, N); label("$c$", (A + B)/2, S); [/asy] As $\angle ACB$ approaches $180^\circ,$ $c$ approaches $2a,$ so $\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2}$ approaches $\frac{a^2 + a^2}{(2a)^2} = \frac{1}{2}.$ This means $M \le \frac{1}{2}.$ On the other hand, by the triangle inequality, $c < a + b,$ so \[c^2 < (a + b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2.\]By AM-GM, $2ab < a^2 + b^2,$ so \[c^2 < 2a^2 + 2b^2.\]Hence, \[\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2} > \frac{1}{2}.\]Therefore, the largest such constant $M$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Find the greatest constant $M,$ so that \[\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2} > M\]whenever $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the sides of a triangle. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Consider a triangle $ABC$ where $a = b.$ [asy] unitsize (3 cm); pair A, B, C; A = (0,0); B = (2,0); C = (1,0.2); draw(A--B--C--cycle); label("$A$", A, W); label("$B$", B, E); label("$C$", C, N); label("$a$", (B + C)/2, N); label("$a$", (A + C)/2, N); label("$c$", (A + B)/2, S); [/asy] As $\angle ACB$ approaches $180^\circ,$ $c$ approaches $2a,$ so $\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2}$ approaches $\frac{a^2 + a^2}{(2a)^2} = \frac{1}{2}.$ This means $M \le \frac{1}{2}.$ On the other hand, by the triangle inequality, $c < a + b,$ so \[c^2 < (a + b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2.\]By AM-GM, $2ab < a^2 + b^2,$ so \[c^2 < 2a^2 + 2b^2.\]Hence, \[\frac{a^2 + b^2}{c^2} > \frac{1}{2}.\]Therefore, the largest such constant $M$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00
